<p>In addition to its tool for searching <a href="/wiki/Webpage" class="mw-redirect" title="Webpage">web pages</a>, Google also provides services for searching images (<a href="/wiki/Google_Images" title="Google Images">Google Images</a>), <a href="/wiki/Usenet" title="Usenet">Usenet</a> <a href="/wiki/Newsgroup" class="mw-redirect" title="Newsgroup">newsgroups</a>, news websites, videos (<b>Google Videos</b>), <a href="/wiki/Local_search_(Internet)" title="Local search (Internet)">searching by locality</a>, maps, and items for sale online. <b>Google Videos</b> allows searching the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">World Wide Web</a> for video clips.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0g&#95;134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0g-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The service evolved from <a href="/wiki/Google_Video" title="Google Video">Google Video</a>, Google's discontinued video hosting service that also allowed to search the web for video clips.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0g&#95;134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0g-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2012, Google has indexed over 30 trillion web pages, and received 100 billion queries per month.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It also <a href="/wiki/Web_cache" title="Web cache">caches</a> much of the content that it <a href="/wiki/Web_indexing" title="Web indexing">indexes</a>. Google operates other tools and services including <a href="/wiki/Google_News" title="Google News">Google News</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google_Shopping" title="Google Shopping">Google Shopping</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google_Maps" title="Google Maps">Google Maps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google_Custom_Search" class="mw-redirect" title="Google Custom Search">Google Custom Search</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google_Earth" title="Google Earth">Google Earth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google_Docs" title="Google Docs">Google Docs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Picasa" title="Picasa">Picasa</a> (discontinued), <a href="/wiki/Panoramio" title="Panoramio">Panoramio</a> (discontinued), <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google_Translate" title="Google Translate">Google Translate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google_Blog" class="mw-redirect" title="Google Blog">Google Blog</a> Search, and <a href="/wiki/Google_Desktop" title="Google Desktop">Google Desktop</a> Search (discontinued<sup id="cite&#95;ref-spring-clean&#95;136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spring-clean-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>).
</p><p>There are also products available from Google that are not directly search-related. <a href="/wiki/Gmail" title="Gmail">Gmail</a>, for example, is a <a href="/wiki/Webmail" title="Webmail">webmail</a> application, but still includes search features; <a href="/wiki/Google_Browser_Sync" title="Google Browser Sync">Google Browser Sync</a> does not offer any search facilities, although it aims to organize your browsing time.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Energy_consumption">Energy consumption</h3></div>
<p>In 2009, Google claimed that a search query requires altogether about 1&#160;<a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">kJ</a> or 0.0003&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kilowatt_hours" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilowatt hours">kW·h</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which is enough to raise the temperature of one liter of water by 0.24&#160;°C. According to green search engine <a href="/wiki/Ecosia" title="Ecosia">Ecosia</a>, the industry standard for search engines is estimated to be about 0.2&#160;grams of CO<sub>2</sub> emission per search.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Google's 40,000 searches per second translate to 8&#160;kg CO<sub>2</sub> per second or over 252 million kilos of CO<sub>2</sub> per year.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Google_Doodles">Google Doodles</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Google_Doodle" title="Google Doodle">Google Doodle</a></div>
<p>On certain occasions, the <a href="/wiki/Google_logo" title="Google logo">logo</a> on Google's webpage will change to a special version, known as a "Google Doodle". This is a picture, drawing, animation, or interactive game that includes the logo. It is usually done for a special event or day although not all of them are well known.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Clicking on the Doodle links to a string of Google search results about the topic. The first was a reference to the <a href="/wiki/Burning_Man_Festival" class="mw-redirect" title="Burning Man Festival">Burning Man Festival</a> in 1998,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and others have been produced for the birthdays of notable people like <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>, historical events like the interlocking <a href="/wiki/Lego" title="Lego">Lego</a> block's 50th anniversary and holidays like <a href="/wiki/Valentine%27s_Day" title="Valentine&#39;s Day">Valentine's Day</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some Google Doodles have interactivity beyond a simple search, such as the famous "Google Pac-Man" version that appeared on May 21, 2010.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Criticism">Criticism</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Privacy">Privacy</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Privacy_concerns_regarding_Google" class="mw-redirect" title="Privacy concerns regarding Google">Privacy concerns regarding Google</a></div>
<p>In 2012, the US <a href="/wiki/Federal_Trade_Commission" title="Federal Trade Commission">Federal Trade Commission</a> fined Google <a href="/wiki/US$" class="mw-redirect" title="US$">US$</a>22.5 million for violating their agreement not to violate the privacy of users of Apple's <a href="/wiki/Safari_(web_browser)" title="Safari (web browser)">Safari web browser</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The FTC was also continuing to investigate if Google's favoring of their own services in their search results violated antitrust regulations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Since 2012, Google Inc. has globally introduced encrypted connections for most of its clients to bypass governative blockings of commercial and IT services.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>146<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Google has been criticized for placing long-term <a href="/wiki/HTTP_cookie" title="HTTP cookie">cookies</a> on users' machines to store preferences, a tactic which also enables them to track a user's search terms and retain the data for more than a year.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ac&#95;147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ac-147"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>147<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Google searches have also triggered <a href="/wiki/Reverse_search_warrant#Keyword_warrant" title="Reverse search warrant">keyword warrants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geofence_warrant" title="Geofence warrant">geofence warrants</a> in which information is shared with law enforcement, leading to a criminal case.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Investigators can request Google to disclose everyone who searched a keyword or query  or every phone in a particular place at a certain time.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2023, the <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Supreme_Court" title="Colorado Supreme Court">Colorado Supreme Court</a> upheld the use of search history requests to identify suspects in a 2020 arson, later stating that it is not a "broad proclamation" and noted that the warrant did not have an individualized probable cause.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>150<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Complaints_about_indexing">Complaints about indexing</h3></div>
<p>In 2003, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> complained about Google's <a href="/wiki/Search_engine_indexing" title="Search engine indexing">indexing</a>, claiming that Google's <a href="/wiki/Web_cache" title="Web cache">caching</a> of content on its site infringed its copyright for the content.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In both <i><a href="/wiki/Field_v._Google" class="mw-redirect" title="Field v. Google">Field v. Google</a></i> and <i>Parker v. Google</i>, the United States <a href="/wiki/Nevada_District_Courts" title="Nevada District Courts">District Court of Nevada</a> ruled in favor of Google.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>152<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>153<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Child_sexual_abuse">Child sexual abuse</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1305433154" /><table class="box-Expand&#95;section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/60px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" decoding="async" width="44" height="31" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/120px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <span class="anonymous-show"><span class="plainlinks"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Talk%3AGoogle_Search&amp;preload=Template%3ASubmit+an+edit+request%2Fpreload&amp;action=edit&amp;section=new&amp;editintro=Template%3AEdit+fully-protected%2Feditintro&amp;preloadtitle=Protected+edit+request+on+26+February+2026&amp;preloadparams%5B%5D=edit+fully-protected&amp;preloadparams%5B%5D=Google+Search">making an edit request</a></span></span><span class="user-show"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Google_Search&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding missing information</a></span>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">May 2024</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A 2019 <i>New York Times</i> article on Google Search showed that images of <a href="/wiki/Child_sexual_abuse" title="Child sexual abuse">child sexual abuse</a> had been found on Google and that the company had been reluctant at times to remove them.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>154<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="January_2009_malware_bug">January 2009 malware bug</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Google_Search_error_of_January_31,_2009.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/87/Google_Search_error_of_January_31%2C_2009.png/250px-Google_Search_error_of_January_31%2C_2009.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="169" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/87/Google_Search_error_of_January_31%2C_2009.png/500px-Google_Search_error_of_January_31%2C_2009.png 1.5x" data-file-width="897" data-file-height="606" /></a><figcaption>A screenshot of the error of January 31, 2009</figcaption></figure>
<p>Google flags search results with the message "This site may harm your computer" if the site is known to install malicious software in the background or otherwise surreptitiously. For approximately 40 minutes on January 31, 2009, all search results were mistakenly classified as <a href="/wiki/Malware" title="Malware">malware</a> and could therefore not be clicked; instead a warning message was displayed and the user was required to enter the requested URL manually. The bug was caused by human error.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>155<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-googleblog&#95;156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-googleblog-156"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>156<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-stopbadware&#95;157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stopbadware-157"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>157<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>158<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>&#160;The <a href="/wiki/URL" title="URL">URL</a> of "/" (which expands to all URLs) was mistakenly added to the malware patterns file.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-googleblog&#95;156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-googleblog-156"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>156<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-stopbadware&#95;157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stopbadware-157"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>157<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Possible_misuse_of_search_results">Possible misuse of search results</h3></div>
<p>In 2007, a group of researchers observed a tendency for users to rely exclusively on Google Search for finding information, writing that "With the Google interface the user gets the impression that the search results imply a kind of totality. In fact, one only sees a small part of what one could see if one also integrates other research tools."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>159<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011, Google Search query results have been shown by Internet activist <a href="/wiki/Eli_Pariser" title="Eli Pariser">Eli Pariser</a> to be tailored to users, effectively isolating users in what he defined as a <a href="/wiki/Filter_bubble" title="Filter bubble">filter bubble</a>. Pariser holds algorithms used in search engines such as Google Search responsible for catering "a personal ecosystem of information".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>160<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Although contrasting views have mitigated the potential threat of "informational dystopia" and questioned the scientific nature of Pariser's claims,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>161<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> filter bubbles have been mentioned to account for the surprising results of the <a href="/wiki/2016_United_States_elections" title="2016 United States elections">U.S. presidential election in 2016</a> alongside <a href="/wiki/Fake_news" title="Fake news">fake news</a> and <a href="/wiki/Echo_chamber_(media)" title="Echo chamber (media)">echo chambers</a>, suggesting that <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a> and Google have designed personalized online realities in which "we only see and hear what we like".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>162<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Payments_to_Apple">Payments to Apple</h3></div>
<p>In a November 2023 disclosure, during the ongoing antitrust trial against Google, an economics professor at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a> revealed that Google pays Apple 36% of all search advertising revenue generated when users access Google through the Safari browser. This revelation reportedly caused Google's lead attorney to cringe visibly.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>163<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The revenue generated from Safari users has been kept confidential, but the 36% figure suggests that it is likely in the tens of billions of dollars.
</p><p>Both Apple and Google have argued that disclosing the specific terms of their search default agreement would harm their competitive positions. However, the court ruled that the information was relevant to the antitrust case and ordered its disclosure. This revelation has raised concerns about the dominance of Google in the search engine market and the potential anticompetitive effects of its agreements with Apple.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>164<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Big_data_and_human_bias">Big data and human bias</h3></div>
<p>Google <a href="/wiki/Web_search_engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Web search engine">search engine</a> robots are programmed to use <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithms</a> that understand and predict human <a href="/wiki/Behavior" title="Behavior">behavior</a>. The book, <i>Race After Technology: Abolitionist Tools for the New Jim Code</i><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-165"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>165<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> by <a href="/wiki/Ruha_Benjamin" title="Ruha Benjamin">Ruha Benjamin</a> talks about human <a href="/wiki/Bias" title="Bias">bias</a> as a behavior that the Google search engine can recognize. In 2016, some users Google searched "three Black teenagers" and images of criminal <a href="/wiki/Mug_shot" title="Mug shot">mugshots</a> of young African American teenagers came up. Then, the users searched "three White teenagers" and were presented with photos of smiling, happy teenagers. They also searched for "three Asian teenagers", and very revealing photos of Asian girls and women appeared. Benjamin concluded that these results reflect human <a href="/wiki/Prejudice" title="Prejudice">prejudice</a> and views on different <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic group">ethnic groups</a>. A group of analysts explained the concept of a <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racist</a> computer program: "The idea here is that computers, unlike people, can't be racist but we're increasingly learning that they do in fact take after their makers&#160;... Some experts believe that this problem might stem from the hidden biases in the massive piles of <a href="/wiki/Data" title="Data">data</a> that the algorithms process as they learn to recognize patterns&#160;... reproducing our worst values".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-165"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>165<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Monopoly_ruling">Monopoly ruling</h3></div>
<p>On August 5, 2024, Google lost a <a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Google_LLC_(2020)" title="United States v. Google LLC (2020)">lawsuit which started in 2020</a> in <a href="/wiki/D.C._Circuit_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="D.C. Circuit Court">D.C. Circuit Court</a>, with Judge <a href="/wiki/Amit_Mehta" title="Amit Mehta">Amit Mehta</a> finding that the company had an illegal monopoly over Internet search.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>166<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This monopoly was held to be in violation of Section 2 of the <a href="/wiki/Sherman_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Sherman Act">Sherman Act</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>167<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Google has said it will appeal the ruling,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>168<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> though they did propose to loosen search deals with Apple and others requiring them to set Google as the default search engine.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>169<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Trademark">Trademark</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Google_(verb)" title="Google (verb)">Google (verb)</a></div>
<p>As people talk about "googling" rather than searching, the company has taken some steps to defend its trademark, in an effort to prevent it from becoming a <a href="/wiki/Generic_trademark" title="Generic trademark">generic trademark</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>170<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>171<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This has led to lawsuits, threats of lawsuits, and the use of euphemisms, such as calling Google Search a famous web search engine.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>172<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Discontinued_features">Discontinued features</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Translate_foreign_pages">Translate foreign pages</h3></div>
<p>Until May 2013, Google Search had offered a feature to <a href="/wiki/Cross-language_information_retrieval" title="Cross-language information retrieval">translate search queries into other languages</a>. A Google spokesperson told <i><a href="/wiki/Search_Engine_Land" class="mw-redirect" title="Search Engine Land">Search Engine Land</a></i> that "Removing features is always tough, but we do think very hard about each decision and its implications for our users. Unfortunately, this feature never saw much pick up".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>173<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Instant_search"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1238216509">.mw-parser-output .vanchor>:target~.vanchor-text{background-color:#b1d2ff}@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .vanchor>:target~.vanchor-text{background-color:#0f4dc9}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .vanchor>:target~.vanchor-text{background-color:#0f4dc9}}</style><span class="vanchor"><span id="Instant&#95;search"></span><span id="Instant&#95;Search"></span><span class="vanchor-text">Instant search</span></span></h3></div>
<p>Instant search was announced in September 2010 as a feature that <a href="/wiki/Incremental_search" title="Incremental search">displayed suggested results while the user typed in their search query</a>, initially only in select countries or to registered users.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>174<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The primary advantage of the new system was its ability to save time, with <a href="/wiki/Marissa_Mayer" title="Marissa Mayer">Marissa Mayer</a>, then-vice president of search products and user experience, proclaiming that the feature would save 2–5 seconds per search, elaborating that "That may not seem like a lot at first, but it adds up. With Google Instant, we estimate that we'll save our users 11 hours with each passing second!"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>175<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Matt Van Wagner of <i><a href="/wiki/Search_Engine_Land" class="mw-redirect" title="Search Engine Land">Search Engine Land</a></i> wrote that "Personally, I kind of like Google Instant and I think it represents a natural evolution in the way search works", and also praised Google's efforts in <a href="/wiki/Public_relations" title="Public relations">public relations</a>, writing that "With just a press conference and a few well-placed interviews, Google has parlayed this relatively minor speed improvement into an attention-grabbing front-page news story".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>176<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The upgrade also became notable for the company switching Google Search's underlying technology from <a href="/wiki/HTML" title="HTML">HTML</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ajax_(programming)" title="Ajax (programming)">AJAX</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>177<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Instant Search could be disabled via Google's "preferences" menu for those who didn't want its functionality.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>178<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The publication <i><a href="/wiki/2600:_The_Hacker_Quarterly" title="2600: The Hacker Quarterly">2600: The Hacker Quarterly</a></i> compiled a list of words that Google Instant did not show suggested results for, with a Google spokesperson giving the following statement to <i><a href="/wiki/Mashable" title="Mashable">Mashable</a></i>:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>179<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>There are several reasons you may not be seeing search queries for a particular topic. Among other things, we apply a narrow set of removal policies for pornography, violence, and hate speech. It's important to note that removing queries from Autocomplete is a hard problem, and not as simple as blacklisting particular terms and phrases.
</p><p>In search, we get more than one billion searches each day. Because of this, we take an algorithmic approach to removals, and just like our search algorithms, these are imperfect. We will continue to work to improve our approach to removals in Autocomplete, and are listening carefully to feedback from our users.
</p><p>
Our algorithms look not only at specific words, but compound queries based on those words, and across all languages. So, for example, if there's a bad word in Russian, we may remove a compound word including the transliteration of the Russian word into English. We also look at the search results themselves for given queries. So, for example, if the results for a particular query seem pornographic, our algorithms may remove that query from Autocomplete, even if the query itself wouldn't otherwise violate our policies. This system is neither perfect nor instantaneous, and we will continue to work to make it better.</p></blockquote>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/PC_Magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="PC Magazine">PC Magazine</a></i> discussed the inconsistency in how some forms of the same topic are allowed; for instance, "lesbian" was blocked, while "gay" was not, and "cocaine" was blocked, while "crack" and "heroin" were not. The report further stated that seemingly normal words were also blocked due to pornographic innuendos, most notably "scat", likely due to having two completely separate contextual meanings, one for music and one for a sexual practice.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>180<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>On July 26, 2017, Google removed Instant results, due to a growing number of searches on mobile devices, where interaction with search, as well as screen sizes, differ significantly from a computer.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>181<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>182<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Instant_previews"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238216509" /><span class="vanchor"><span id="Instant&#95;previews"></span><span id="Instant&#95;previews"></span><span class="vanchor-text">Instant previews</span></span></h3></div>
<p>"Instant previews" allowed previewing screenshots of search results' web pages without having to open them. Clicking on the magnifying glass beside the search-result links will show a screenshot of the web page and highlight the image's relevant text. Google said that the feature "helps people find information faster by showing a visual preview of each result." The snapshots of web pages are stored on Google's servers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-183"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>183<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The feature was introduced in November 2010 to the desktop website and removed in April 2013, citing low usage.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-183"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>183<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>184<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Dedicated_encrypted_search_page">Dedicated encrypted search page</h3></div>
<p>Various search engines provide encrypted Web search facilities. In May 2010 Google rolled out SSL-encrypted web search.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-sslsearch01&#95;185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sslsearch01-185"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>185<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The encrypted search was accessed at <code>encrypted.google.com</code><sup id="cite&#95;ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>186<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, the web search is encrypted via <a href="/wiki/Transport_Layer_Security" title="Transport Layer Security">Transport Layer Security</a> (TLS) by default today, thus every search request should be automatically encrypted if TLS is supported by the web browser.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>187<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On its support website, Google announced that the address <code>encrypted.google.com</code> would be turned off April 30, 2018, stating that all Google products and most new browsers use HTTPS connections as the reason for the discontinuation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>188<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Real-Time_Search">Real-Time Search</h3></div>
<p>Google Real-Time Search was a feature of Google Search in which search results also sometimes included <a href="/wiki/Real-time_computing" title="Real-time computing">real-time</a> information from sources such as <a href="/wiki/Twitter" class="mw-redirect" title="Twitter">Twitter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blog" title="Blog">blogs</a>, and news websites.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mashablelaunch&#95;189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mashablelaunch-189"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>189<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The feature was introduced on December 7, 2009,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>190<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and went offline on July 2, 2011, after the deal with Twitter expired.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>191<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Real-Time Search included <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a> status updates beginning on February 24, 2010.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>192<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A feature similar to Real-Time Search was already available on <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Bing_(search_engine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bing (search engine)">Bing search engine</a>, which showed results from <a href="/wiki/Twitter" class="mw-redirect" title="Twitter">Twitter</a> and Facebook.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>193<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The interface for the engine showed a live, descending "river" of posts in the main region (which could be paused or resumed), while a <a href="/wiki/Bar_chart" title="Bar chart">bar chart</a> metric of the frequency of posts containing a certain search term or hashtag was located on the right hand corner of the page above a list of most frequently reposted posts and outgoing links. <a href="/wiki/Hashtag" title="Hashtag">Hashtag</a> search links were also supported, as were "promoted" tweets hosted by Twitter (located persistently on top of the river) and thumbnails of retweeted image or video links.
</p><p>In January 2011, geolocation links of posts were made available alongside results in Real-Time Search. In addition, posts containing syndicated or attached shortened links were made searchable by the <i>link:</i> query option. In July 2011, Real-Time Search became inaccessible, with the Real-Time link in the Google sidebar disappearing and a custom 404 error page generated by Google returned at its former URL. Google originally suggested that the interruption was temporary and related to the launch of <a href="/wiki/Google%2B" title="Google+">Google+</a>;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>194<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> they subsequently announced that it was due to the expiry of a commercial arrangement with Twitter to provide access to tweets.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>195<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
